	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Bonelli's eagle</h1>
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>

<p>The <b>Bonelli's eagle</b> (<i>Aquila fasciata</i>) is a large <a href="/wiki/Bird_of_prey" title="Bird of prey">bird of prey</a>. The common name of the bird commemorates the Italian ornithologist and collector <a href="/wiki/Franco_Andrea_Bonelli" title="Franco Andrea Bonelli">Franco Andrea Bonelli</a>. Bonelli is credited with gathering the type specimen, most likely from an exploration of <a href="/wiki/Sardinia" title="Sardinia">Sardinia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Beolens2003_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beolens2003-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Aimassi_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aimassi-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Some antiquated texts also refer to this species as the <b>crestless hawk-eagle</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Like all <a href="/wiki/Eagle" title="Eagle">eagles</a>, Bonelli's eagle belongs to the family <a href="/wiki/Accipitridae" title="Accipitridae">Accipitridae</a>. Its feathered legs marked it as member of the <a href="/wiki/Aquilinae" title="Aquilinae">Aquilinae</a> or booted eagle subfamily.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> This species breeds from southern Europe, Africa on the montane perimeter of the <a href="/wiki/Sahara_Desert" class="mw-redirect" title="Sahara Desert">Sahara Desert</a> and across the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Subcontinent" class="mw-redirect" title="Indian Subcontinent">Indian Subcontinent</a> to <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>. On the great Eurasian continent, this species may be found as far west as <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> and as far east as <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Southeastern China">southeastern China</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>. It is usually a resident breeder.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The Bonelli's eagle is often found in hilly or mountainous habitats, with rocky walls or crags, from sea level to 1,500&#160;m (4,900&#160;ft). Habitats are often open to wooded land and can occur in arid to semi-moist climate.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> This eagle, though it can be considered partially opportunistic, is something of a special predator of certain <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/European_rabbit" title="European rabbit">rabbits</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galliformes" title="Galliformes">galliforms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pigeon" class="mw-redirect" title="Pigeon">pigeons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ontiveros_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ontiveros-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> On evidence, when staple prey populations decline or are locally scarce, Bonelli's eagle switch to being an opportunistic predator of a wide variety of birds.<sup id="cite_ref-Moleon_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moleon-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Despite its persistence over a large range and its continued classification as a <a href="/wiki/Least_concern_species" class="mw-redirect" title="Least concern species">least concern species</a> by the <a href="/wiki/IUCN" class="mw-redirect" title="IUCN">IUCN</a>, the Bonelli's eagle has declined precipitously in various parts of its range, including almost all of its European distribution, and may face potential <a href="/wiki/Local_extinction" title="Local extinction">local extinction</a>. The species' declines are due to widespread <a href="/wiki/Habitat_destruction" title="Habitat destruction">habitat destruction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electrocution" title="Electrocution">electrocution</a> from <a href="/wiki/Electricity_pylon" class="mw-redirect" title="Electricity pylon">electricity pylons</a> as well as persistent <a href="/wiki/Persecution" title="Persecution">persecution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lopez-Lopez_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lopez-Lopez-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sanchez-Alonso_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sanchez-Alonso-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bonelli%27s_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Taxonomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Bonelli's eagle was described in 1822 by French ornithologist <a href="/wiki/Louis_Jean_Pierre_Vieillot" title="Louis Jean Pierre Vieillot">Louis Jean Pierre Vieillot</a>. The common name Bonelli's eagle is for the collector of the type specimen, <a href="/wiki/Franco_Andrea_Bonelli" title="Franco Andrea Bonelli">Franco Andrea Bonelli</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Beolens2003_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beolens2003-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Aimassi_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aimassi-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Bonelli's eagle is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Aquilinae" title="Aquilinae">Aquilinae</a> or booted eagles, a <a href="/wiki/Monophyly" title="Monophyly">monophyletic</a> subfamily of the accipitrid family. At least 38 species are currently housed in the subfamily, all with signature well-feathered tarsi.<sup id="cite_ref-Lerner_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lerner-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/African_hawk-eagle" title="African hawk-eagle">African hawk-eagle</a> (<i>Aquila spilogaster</i>) was once <a href="/wiki/Lumpers_and_splitters#Lumping_and_splitting_in_biology" title="Lumpers and splitters">lumped</a> with the Bonelli's eagle, with most accounts until about the 1990s listing the species as monotypical.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> However, several morphological differences between the two species, life history discrepancies and their considerably <a href="/wiki/Allopatric_speciation" title="Allopatric speciation">allopatric distribution</a> lead them to being considered separate species. Despite the differences between the Bonelli's eagle and the African hawk-eagle the two species are visibly similar and are still considered <a href="/wiki/Species_complex" title="Species complex">sister species</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> However, genetic studies have indicated that they are not closely related relative to other species pairs of the booted eagle subfamily.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Recent <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> research resulted in the two species being moved, in 2014,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> to the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Aquila_(genus)" title="Aquila (genus)">Aquila</a></i> from <i><a href="/wiki/Hieraaetus" title="Hieraaetus">Hieraaetus</a></i>, along with another dissimilar species, the <a href="/wiki/Cassin%27s_hawk-eagle" title="Cassin&#39;s hawk-eagle">Cassin's hawk-eagle</a> (<i>Aquila africana</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Helbig_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helbig-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> More specifically and surprisingly, Bonelli's, African hawk- and Cassin's hawk-eagles were found to be genetically closely related to the <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagle</a> (<i>Aquila chrysaetos</i>) <a href="/wiki/Species_complex" title="Species complex">species complex</a>, which also includes <a href="/wiki/Verreaux%27s_eagle" title="Verreaux&#39;s eagle">Verreaux's eagle</a> (<i>Aquila verreauxii</i>), <a href="/wiki/Gurney%27s_eagle" title="Gurney&#39;s eagle">Gurney's eagle</a> (<i>Aquila gurneyi</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Wedge-tailed_eagle" title="Wedge-tailed eagle">wedge-tailed eagle</a> (<i>Aquila audax</i>). These species are all conspicuously larger than the Bonelli's and African hawk-eagles with differing proportions to their wings, tail and legs (in adaptation to their open country habits) and much darker coloured plumages. Furthermore, the four other traditional members of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Aquila_(genus)" title="Aquila (genus)">Aquila</a></i> have been revealed to be a separate <a href="/wiki/Species_complex" title="Species complex">species complex</a> despite showing superficial similarity to the golden eagle group, i.e. being relatively large and long winged with usually dark coloring.<sup id="cite_ref-Lerner_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lerner-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Helbig_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helbig-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Watson_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lerner2_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lerner2-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Beyond the nominate subspecies of Bonelli's eagle, which is found throughout its range in Eurasia, a second subspecies dwells in the <a href="/wiki/Lesser_Sunda_Islands" title="Lesser Sunda Islands">Lesser Sunda Islands</a>, <i>A. f. renschi</i>. The latter race is linearly smaller, and compared to other Bonelli's eagles tends to have more strikingly barred remiges and tail, the belly, thighs and crissum more boldly marked. At one time, its restricted and very isolated range have caused authors to suggest <i>A. f. renschi</i> may be a full species but recent studies have indicated that it is not genetically distinct enough to be considered a separate species. Furthermore, the most recent analysis couldn't rule out early introductions (possibly by ancient <a href="/wiki/Falconry" title="Falconry">falconers</a>) at least playing a part in the species presence in the Lesser Sundas, as some other established wild birds on those islands are certain to have reached there by early human introductions.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Trainor_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Trainor-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bonelli%27s_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Size_and_form">Size and form</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bonelli%27s_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Size and form">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bonelli%27s_Eagle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Bonelli%27s_Eagle.jpg/220px-Bonelli%27s_Eagle.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Bonelli%27s_Eagle.jpg/330px-Bonelli%27s_Eagle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Bonelli%27s_Eagle.jpg/440px-Bonelli%27s_Eagle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="473" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bonelli%27s_Eagle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Perched on a tree near a wetland in <a href="/wiki/Biligiriranga_Hills" title="Biligiriranga Hills">Biligiri Rangaswamy Temple Tiger Reserve</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Bonelli's eagle is a fairly large bird of prey and a medium-sized eagle. When still classified as a member of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Hieraaetus" title="Hieraaetus">Hieraaetus</a></i>, it was considered the largest extant species therein, however, as a member of <i><a href="/wiki/Aquila_(genus)" title="Aquila (genus)">Aquila</a></i> it is amongst the smallest-bodied species.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Amongst the currently accepted species of <i>Aquila</i> eagles, it is of similar size to the <a href="/wiki/Tawny_eagle" title="Tawny eagle">tawny eagle</a> (<i>Aquila rapax</i>), slightly larger than the <a href="/wiki/African_hawk-eagle" title="African hawk-eagle">African hawk-eagle</a> and notably larger than the <a href="/wiki/Cassin%27s_hawk-eagle" title="Cassin&#39;s hawk-eagle">Cassin's hawk-eagle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CRC_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Like most birds of prey, the Bonelli's eagle displays <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">reverse sexual dimorphism</a> as the female is larger than the male to the contrary of most other kinds of birds, in this case she may average about 10% larger overall.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Total length in fully-grown eagles of the species can vary from 55 to 74&#160;cm (22 to 29&#160;in). Wingspan in males can vary from 143 to 163&#160;cm (4&#160;ft 8&#160;in to 5&#160;ft 4&#160;in) while that of the female may vary from 156 to 180&#160;cm (5&#160;ft 1&#160;in to 5&#160;ft 11&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Garcia_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Garcia-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clark_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Prior claims put the weight of this species as 1.4 to 2.4&#160;kg (3.1 to 5.3&#160;lb), however this probably slightly underrepresents both their size and the sexual dimorphism of this eagle.<sup id="cite_ref-Clark_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> A large sample of full grown males from <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">western Europe</a> were found to average 1.94&#160;kg (4.3&#160;lb), with a range of 1.4 to 2.24&#160;kg (3.1 to 4.9&#160;lb) (sample size of 91), while 87 females were found to average 2.62&#160;kg (5.8&#160;lb), with a range of 2.1 to 3.03&#160;kg (4.6 to 6.7&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Garcia_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Garcia-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Mature males from western Europe were found to have averaged 65&#160;cm (26&#160;in) in total length and 155&#160;cm (5&#160;ft 1&#160;in) in wingspan while mature females averaged 70.7&#160;cm (27.8&#160;in) in total length and 167.8&#160;cm (5&#160;ft 6&#160;in) in wingspan.<sup id="cite_ref-Garcia_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Garcia-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Although the linear measurements reportedly increase slightly in average size in the eastern <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asian</a> part of the range, body weight was similar or slightly lower at a mean of 1.5&#160;kg (3.3&#160;lb) and 2.5&#160;kg (5.5&#160;lb) for males and females from the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>, respectively, though the sample size is unknown in this case.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aguila_perdicera.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Aguila_perdicera.jpg/220px-Aguila_perdicera.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Aguila_perdicera.jpg/330px-Aguila_perdicera.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Aguila_perdicera.jpg/440px-Aguila_perdicera.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1939" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aguila_perdicera.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bonelli's eagles are medium-sized eagles and have distinctly elongated legs and massive feet and talons.</div></div></div>
<p>Bonelli's eagles in general form appear to have a medium-sized head on a fairly long neck, a strong bill, a mid-to-longish tail and exceptionally long and well feathered legs. The combination of its well-proportioned, stout body and elongated legs may lend to descriptions of the species as “athletic” in appearance.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rasmussen_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rasmussen-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> This eagle often perches with a very upright carriage, at times openly on a rock, a crag, tree branches or some form of post but also in the  foliage of tree cover, especially when actively hunting. When perched, the wing tips tend to fall a bit short of the tail tip.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Among standard linear measurements, the <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">wing chord</a> of males varies from 458 to 542&#160;mm (18.0 to 21.3&#160;in), with an average in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">western Europe</a> of 480.4&#160;mm (18.91&#160;in), in <a href="/wiki/Tail" title="Tail">tail</a> length from 237 to 287&#160;mm (9.3 to 11.3&#160;in), with an average of 268.1&#160;mm (10.56&#160;in), in <a href="/wiki/Tibiotarsus" title="Tibiotarsus">tarsus</a> length from 93 to 120&#160;mm (3.7 to 4.7&#160;in), with an average of 99.5&#160;mm (3.92&#160;in) and in <a href="/wiki/Beak" title="Beak">total bill</a> length from 40.4 to 45.3&#160;mm (1.59 to 1.78&#160;in), with an average of 43.3&#160;mm (1.70&#160;in). Meanwhile, females vary in wing chord from 478 to 560&#160;mm (18.8 to 22.0&#160;in), in tail length from 246 to 319&#160;mm (9.7 to 12.6&#160;in), with an average of 288.5&#160;mm (11.36&#160;in), in tarsus length from 93 to 127&#160;mm (3.7 to 5.0&#160;in), with an average of 119.1&#160;mm (4.69&#160;in), and in total bill length from 41.3 to 51.8&#160;mm (1.63 to 2.04&#160;in), with an average of 46.6&#160;mm (1.83&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Garcia_26-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Garcia-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ali_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ali-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Two males from the <i>A. f. renschi</i> race measured 444 and 452&#160;mm (17.5 and 17.8&#160;in) in wing chord length and a single female measured 493&#160;mm (19.4&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The Bonelli's eagle is intermediate in its wing lengths and tail length proportionately between the shorter-tailed and longer-winged eagles of open country and longer-tailed and shorter-winged forest eagles, which allows to vary its hunting between short-burst, agile surprise attacks in trees and ample ground-covering pursuits in the open.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Whistler_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whistler-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown2_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown2-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Its talons and feet are proportionately very large and presumably rather powerful for the eagle's size. In particular the elongated talon on its rear toe (used as a killing apparatus by almost all accipitrids), or <a href="/wiki/Claw" title="Claw">hallux claw</a>, is longer than that of the much larger <a href="/wiki/Eastern_imperial_eagle" title="Eastern imperial eagle">eastern imperial eagle</a> (<i>Aquila heliaca</i>) and proportionately slightly larger even than its powerful <a href="/wiki/Sympatry" title="Sympatry">sympatric competitor</a>, the twice as massive <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Hallux claw lengths in Bonelli's eagles from <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">western Europe</a> averaged 37.21&#160;mm (1.465&#160;in) in males and in females averaged 43.1&#160;mm (1.70&#160;in), and could farther range up to 47&#160;mm (1.9&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Garcia_26-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Garcia-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colouring_and_identification">Colouring and identification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bonelli%27s_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Colouring and identification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Juvenile_Bonelli%27s_eagle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Juvenile_Bonelli%27s_eagle.jpg/220px-Juvenile_Bonelli%27s_eagle.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Juvenile_Bonelli%27s_eagle.jpg/330px-Juvenile_Bonelli%27s_eagle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Juvenile_Bonelli%27s_eagle.jpg/440px-Juvenile_Bonelli%27s_eagle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="633" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Juvenile_Bonelli%27s_eagle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Juvenile Bonelli's eagle in flight, in rural <a href="/wiki/Sangli_district" title="Sangli district">Sangli district</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Adult are dark brown above, from a cold hue similar to <a href="/wiki/Dark_chocolate" title="Dark chocolate">dark chocolate</a> to an <a href="/wiki/Umber" title="Umber">umber</a> hue depending on their state of molt as well as to some extent individual and regional variances, with pale margins to most feathers. These pale margins are especially broad on the median wing coverts (which thus appear lighter brown overall). Adults also have a variably-sized, irregular white patch on the mantle that can vary from nearly absent (though almost never completely so) to being quite large and extending to the upper back. The adult's tail is grey with obscure darker brown thin bars, with a broad blackish subterminal band and creamy white tip. The adult Bonelli's head is dark brown with a paler, often streaky neck and a white throat. The underside has a cream base colour with variable amounts of sparse blackish-brown streaks or drop shaped markings.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clark_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ali2_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ali2-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The adult female averages darker and more heavily patterned than the adult male, particular on the underside, a case of colour sexual dimorphism otherwise seemingly rare in <a href="/wiki/Aquilinae" title="Aquilinae">booted eagles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Garcia_26-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Garcia-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The streaking on this eagle is normally strongest on the breast and upper flanks while the lower belly and crissum are typically either plain or only faintly marked. Juveniles are a lighter medium brown above with variable paler edges, sometimes with a creamy patch on the back (not the mantle as in the adults) and uppertail coverts.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clark_27-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ali2_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ali2-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Generally, juveniles have a rusty-brown head with a darker brown around and behind their eyes. The juvenile eagle's crown is either darkly streaked or, occasionally, plain greyish. The tail of young birds is more clearly barred than the adults while the subterminal band is only negligibly thicker than the other bars. Like adults, the juvenile Bonelli's eagle's tail has a thin white tip. The juvenile is light rufous to warm buff below with minimal black streaks, which are normally confined to chest-sides.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clark_27-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsman_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sven_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sven-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> By their 2nd summer, the young eagles are still largely the same in colouring but tend to become more patchy below with increased heavy streaking. During the gradual further development through subsequent molts, the immature eagles develop a thicker subterminal band and a paler ground colour below. Among the bare parts, adult's eyes are yellow to yellow-orange while those of the juvenile are hazel-brown. Adult plumage is obtained between the 4th and 5th years. At all ages, the cere and feet are both pale yellow.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clark_27-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In flight, the Bonelli's eagle is a largish raptor with a well projecting head and broad, long and somewhat square ended wings which are slightly pinched in at body with a little tapering at tips. Feather molts can make the wings look quite variable in shape with some individuals appearing rather longer and narrower winged than others. In flight, the tail appears long and broad but if pinched in can appear surprisingly narrow.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsman_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> This species tends to fly with powerful but loose shallow beats. When gliding, they do so often on flat wings with well-spread feathers and the carpals pressed slightly forward but more so when entering a fast glide. This species soars infrequently on flat or slightly raised wings.<sup id="cite_ref-Porter_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Porter-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> At nearly all times of the year, Bonelli's eagles quite often flies in pairs.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> In colouring, the flying adult is dark above with a variable amount of the white marking on the mantle. The tail has faded barring (rarely perceptible) on grey with a big blotchy subterminal band and a white tip above. The markings on the tail look more or less the same when seen both from below and above. Adult Bonelli's eagles have white lesser coverts which along with the greyish tail stand out in contrast against blackish central wing band over the greater and median coverts. Also the flight feathers are faintly and thinly barred light grey-brown with paler bases, which often become paler (to a whitish hue) on the primaries inside blackish tips and leading wing coverts. In flight, juveniles are brown above with slightly darker wing ends and tips to greater coverts and greyer primary windows. Occasionally, juveniles manifest a creamy patch on back and obscure narrow U above barred tail, which even if present are only sometimes visible. Below the juvenile's wing linings are light rufous to warm buff like the body colour. Usually juveniles appear with darker tips to greater coverts forming wing-diagonals (sometimes lacking or confined to carpal area) and a small but distinct area of white on primaries against the blackish tips. Until the 3rd year, the young eagles appear more like a 1st year juvenile than an adult, but begin developing more streaks below and darker greater underwing coverts. By the 4th year, the subadult Bonelli's are increasingly similar to the adult, with an increasing subterminal band, a whiter underbody and fairly prominent underwing-diagonals. However, subadults are often still appear with a mix of paler barred juvenile type feathers and plainer darker adult feathers on the flight feathers.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clark_27-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsman_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Porter_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Porter-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_Fasciata.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Aquila_Fasciata.jpg/220px-Aquila_Fasciata.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Aquila_Fasciata.jpg/330px-Aquila_Fasciata.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Aquila_Fasciata.jpg/440px-Aquila_Fasciata.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="1440" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_Fasciata.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An adult Bonelli's eagle in flight shows its ample white mantle.</div></div></div>
<p>Bonelli's eagles are generally unmistakable when shape, flight and plumage are considered in combination.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> In poor light, it is possible to mistake one with a <a href="/wiki/Pernis_(bird)" title="Pernis (bird)">honey buzzard</a>, one mainly in <a href="/wiki/European_honey_buzzard" title="European honey buzzard">Europe</a> and another mainly in <a href="/wiki/Crested_honey_buzzard" title="Crested honey buzzard">Asia</a>, as these raptors are extremely polymorphic and can come surprisingly close to approximating the plumage of various more powerful raptors. The wing shape in Bonelli's eagles can at times appear similar to that of honey buzzard but the latter raptor type are usually distinctly slimmer and slighter bodied with a much smaller, slimmer head. In flight, honey buzzards often have notched rather than square ended tails, less emarginated primaries and typically they fly with their wings held more at an angle. The sympatric species of honey buzzard tend to have bolder barring on the tail and underwings, broader dark trailing wing edges and all have no pale mantle patch or darker underwing-diagonals.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsman_37-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> An unlikely source of confusion is the <a href="/wiki/Northern_goshawk" title="Northern goshawk">northern goshawk</a> (<i>Accipiter gentilis</i>), which is usually visibly smaller with much shorter wings, a slightly longer tail, different level flight style and many distinctive plumage characteristics. Distant juvenile Bonelli's could conceivably be mistaken for the <a href="/wiki/Long-legged_buzzard" title="Long-legged buzzard">long-legged buzzard</a> (<i>Buteo rufinus</i>), but the buzzard is also smaller and is shorter tailed with prominent dark carpal patches and dark trailing wing edges. Furthermore, the buzzard usually holds its wings in a noticeable <a href="/wiki/Dihedral_(aeronautics)" title="Dihedral (aeronautics)">dihedral</a> when flying in a soar.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsman_37-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Porter_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Porter-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Adult Bonelli's eagles may be mistaken for pale morph adult <a href="/wiki/Booted_eagle" title="Booted eagle">booted eagle</a> (<i>Hieraeetus pennatus</i>) by inexperienced observers but, beyond being a third larger and more than twice as heavy, Bonelli's eagles are moreover distinct in plumage. Overall Bonelli's are much darker and streaker especially compared to the clearly paler flight feathers of booted eagles. Booted eagles are both whiter and more contrasting on the upperwing coverts, have whitish uppertail-coverts and have white on the wing's patagium. More similar in plumage is the juvenile Bonelli's eagle are the rare rufous morph of the booted eagle but the latter can still be told by the booted species’ narrower wings and smaller size.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Porter_39-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Porter-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> In southern part of the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a>, vagrants (largely juveniles) may possibly come into range of the closely related and more similar <a href="/wiki/African_hawk-eagle" title="African hawk-eagle">African hawk-eagle</a>, but the latter is comparatively short winged and longer tailed, with the adult plumage being more contrasting and juveniles being darker above with pale wing-windows.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kemp_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kemp-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocalizations">Vocalizations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bonelli%27s_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Vocalizations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="2.1333333333333" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Bonellis_eagle.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/00/Bonellis_eagle.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (84 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="84221" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/0/00/Bonellis_eagle.ogg/Bonellis_eagle.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="107528" /></audio></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bonellis_eagle.ogg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Call of the Bonelli's eagle.</div></div></div>
<p>Bonelli's eagle is largely silent outside of breeding season and is a somewhat infrequent vocalizer even in the context of breeding. Its calls are less well studied than those of the <a href="/wiki/African_hawk-eagle" title="African hawk-eagle">African hawk-eagle</a>, which although a tropical species, also generally ceases to call outside of the breeding season.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The main call of the Bonelli's eagle is done during the courtship display and, sometimes, also at the nest. Its main call consists of a loud, shrill, somewhat far-carrying scream, <i>yuiii-yuiii-gii-gii</i> or a drawn-oout <i>heeeeii-heeeeii</i> with slight regional or even individual variations. Its call is farther carrying than the “puppy-like” one of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagle</a> and is reminiscent in pitch of that of the <a href="/wiki/Red-tailed_hawk" title="Red-tailed hawk">red-tailed hawk</a> (<i>Buteo jamaicensis</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_12-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sven_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sven-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The call may be given by both sexes. However, the female Bonelli's eagle calls most intensely when the male is delivering prey unlike the preference for vocalizing in aerial display as the male usually does. Other recorded vocalizations have included a fluted, low-pitched <i>klu-klu-klu</i> as well as a repeated <i>ki ki ki</i> in alarm. Also other barking, gurgling and grunting sounds have been reported by researchers at or near the nests.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown2_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown2-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Range_and_habitat">Range and habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bonelli%27s_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Range and habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sierra_de_las_Nieves.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Sierra_de_las_Nieves.jpg/175px-Sierra_de_las_Nieves.jpg" decoding="async" width="175" height="264" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Sierra_de_las_Nieves.jpg/263px-Sierra_de_las_Nieves.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/59/Sierra_de_las_Nieves.jpg 2x" data-file-width="305" data-file-height="460" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sierra_de_las_Nieves.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Typically rugged environment with mixed vegetation that the Bonelli's eagle is often at home in. Here in <a href="/wiki/Andalusia" title="Andalusia">Andalusia</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Sierra_de_las_Nieves" title="Sierra de las Nieves">Sierra de las Nieves</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Bonelli's eagle have a spotty and sparse worldwide distribution currently. The species is distributed in <a href="/wiki/Northwestern_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Northwestern Africa">northwestern Africa</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Atlas" title="Anti-Atlas">Anti-Atlas</a> in <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a> northeasterly through the lower parts of the <a href="/wiki/Atlas_mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Atlas mountains">Atlas mountains</a> in northern <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a> and northern <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a> (and probably formerly northern <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Beyond its African breeding range, the <a href="/wiki/IUCN" class="mw-redirect" title="IUCN">IUCN</a> and others have mapped out a semi-regular wintering range for Bonelli's eagles, in coastal <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">west Africa</a> from southern <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a> down through <a href="/wiki/Western_Sahara" title="Western Sahara">Western Sahara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mauritania" title="Mauritania">Mauritania</a> and northwestern <a href="/wiki/Senegal" title="Senegal">Senegal</a> (rarely also east to <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a>), although little more is reportedly known about this population and its origins and altogether the species is considered largely non-migratory.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, the species has been recorded as a vagrant in <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">east Africa</a> in <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a> as well.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> In southern Europe, they range patchily through different parts of <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> into <a href="/wiki/Southern_France" title="Southern France">southern France</a> as far north as the department of <a href="/wiki/Dr%C3%B4me" title="Drôme">Drôme</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cugnasse_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cugnasse-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Discontinuously, they are now seemingly solely left as breeding bird in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> on the islands of <a href="/wiki/Sardinia" title="Sardinia">Sardinia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lopez-Lopez_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lopez-Lopez-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Southeastern Europe">southeastern Europe</a>, an isolated population possibly persists in <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a> as well as in northern and southern <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(region)" title="Macedonia (region)">Macedonia</a> (with the further possibility of spilling over into <a href="/wiki/Kosovo" title="Kosovo">Kosovo</a>) and spottily through different areas of <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> (possibly spilling over the borders in the west in <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a> and in the east in <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>), as well as <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Out of Europe, they may be found in western and southern <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> (possibly but most likely <a href="/wiki/Local_extinction" title="Local extinction">extirpated</a>), the isle of <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, western <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>, northeastern <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> (rarely in northern half of <a href="/wiki/Sinai_Peninsula" title="Sinai Peninsula">Sinai Peninsula</a>) possibly but not certainly in spots in the west and south of <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> and elsewhere in the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> to <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Oman</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kassinis_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kassinis-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Shirihai_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shirihai-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vaassen_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaassen-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Elsewhere in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>, their range includes eastern <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> and west, south and northeastern <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, extending somewhat into <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Kopet_Dag" title="Kopet Dag">Kopet Dag</a> range.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Further east into Asia, their distribution includes eastern <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> through most of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>, where generally it is uncommon but more locally common near <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>. On the other hand, they are absent in eastern India and only occurring as a vagrant to <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> In India, they are most regularly occur in certain area such as <a href="/wiki/Chambal_river" class="mw-redirect" title="Chambal river">Chambal ravines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ranthambore_National_Park" title="Ranthambore National Park">Ranthambore National Park</a>, Chir zone of lower <a href="/wiki/Kumaon_division" title="Kumaon division">Kumaun Himalayas</a> and in winter in the <a href="/wiki/Keoladeo_National_Park" title="Keoladeo National Park">Keoladeo National Park</a> of <a href="/wiki/Bharatpur,_Rajasthan" title="Bharatpur, Rajasthan">Bharatpur, Rajasthan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> From central <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a> they range across into northwestern <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> and northern <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a> (though possibly only as a visitor rather than breeding in the latter two). In southern China their resident range includes <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">Yunnan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guangxi" title="Guangxi">Guangxi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">Guangdong</a> north to <a href="/wiki/Yangtze_river" class="mw-redirect" title="Yangtze river">Yangtze river</a> as well as rarely into <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Their isolated <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesian</a> population ranges is in the <a href="/wiki/Lesser_Sunda_Islands" title="Lesser Sunda Islands">Lesser Sunda Islands</a>, including at least <a href="/wiki/Sumbawa" title="Sumbawa">Sumbawa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Timor" title="Timor">Timor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wetar" title="Wetar">Wetar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maluku_(province)" title="Maluku (province)">Luang</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flores" title="Flores">Flores</a>, however records show they've turned up on as many as 20 islands in the Lesser Sundas.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Trainor_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Trainor-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Bonelli's eagles are mostly residential throughout their range but juvenile can disperse up to over several hundred kilometers. Sometimes, they are recorded at migration sites and at spots where not known to breed in winter.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Wanderings include around 700&#160;km (430&#160;mi) north of their regular range in France near the coast of <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">English Channel</a>, far from their normal haunts in <a href="/wiki/Regensburg" title="Regensburg">Regensburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> and, probably both from the Italian island populations, to northwestern <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> From their <a href="/wiki/Iberian_peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Iberian peninsula">Iberian peninsula</a> range presumably, vagrants have been reported in the <a href="/wiki/Canary_islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Canary islands">Canary islands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Beyond Sri Lanka, other areas the species has been known to vagrate (or perhaps rarely winter) in Asia have included <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Korean_peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Korean peninsula">Korean peninsula</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cochinchina" title="Cochinchina">Cochinchina</a> in <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, as well as a record in winter 1996 on the isle of <a href="/wiki/Yamdena" title="Yamdena">Yamdena</a>, the latter presumably from the Lesser Sunda population.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Trainor_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Trainor-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Habitat">Habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bonelli%27s_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bonelli's eagles across their wide range tend to dwell in similar habitat types. They are mostly distributed in lands hugging large bodies of water, largely the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a> and northern <a href="/wiki/Indian_ocean" class="mw-redirect" title="Indian ocean">Indian ocean</a>. Also, to a lesser extent, they may live near the coast of the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_ocean" class="mw-redirect" title="Atlantic ocean">Atlantic</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_ocean" class="mw-redirect" title="Pacific ocean">Pacific</a> as well as near the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_sea" class="mw-redirect" title="Caspian sea">Caspian sea</a> inland. Despite often being near seas and oceans they mostly occur in fairly arid habitats and in quite sunny areas. In some parts of Asia though semi-moist habitats may be resided in. Bonelli's eagles prefer rocky areas including lower <a href="/wiki/Mountain" title="Mountain">mountains</a> and <a href="/wiki/Foothills" title="Foothills">foothills</a> with plentiful cliffs, as well as steep sided <a href="/wiki/Canyon" title="Canyon">river valleys and gorges</a>. This species is very skilled at hunting in craggy, irregular rocky terrain.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Baccetti_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baccetti-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Usually, extensive <a href="/wiki/Garrigue" title="Garrigue">garrigue</a>-type habitat such as low bushes or more substantial vegetation such as scattered trees are a common feature of residential ranges but also at times even denser <a href="/wiki/Woodland" title="Woodland">woodlands</a>. Such scrubby areas are key since they generally hold prey concentration in Mediterranean habitats.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Carrascal_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carrascal-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Real_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Real-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> However, excessive ground cover may limit hunting success so scrub is avoided when it becomes too dense.<sup id="cite_ref-Carrascal_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carrascal-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Martinez-Miranzo_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martinez-Miranzo-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> In the Mediterranean region, forests visited by Bonelli's eagles are usually either <a href="/wiki/Pinus" class="mw-redirect" title="Pinus">pine forests</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sclerophyll" title="Sclerophyll">sclerophyll forests</a>. Deep forests are generally avoided, however.<sup id="cite_ref-Real_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Real-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vittorio_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vittorio-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Although Carrascal &amp; Seoane (2009) claimed that <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agricultural areas</a> are generally avoided per their analysis in Spain, Martinez-Miranzo et al. (2016) indicated that the species was showing a growing preference for agricultural <a href="/wiki/Arable_land" title="Arable land">arable land</a> and other human-modified habitats, probably as prey selection has shifted more heavily to <a href="/wiki/Rock_pigeon" class="mw-redirect" title="Rock pigeon">pigeons</a> out of necessity.<sup id="cite_ref-Carrascal_83-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carrascal-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Martinez-Miranzo_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martinez-Miranzo-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> A similar growing preference for arables was also detected in <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Vittorio_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vittorio-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> However, urban areas are generally strongly avoided both as breeding and as foraging areas by this species.<sup id="cite_ref-Real_84-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Real-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Bonelli's eagles may additionally range into <a href="/wiki/Plain" title="Plain">timbered plains</a> or even virtually barren slopes or <a href="/wiki/Semi-arid_climate" title="Semi-arid climate">semi-desert</a>, especially in areas such as <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> and <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> where moister valleys intersect with deserts.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Real_84-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Real-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bahat_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bahat-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Juveniles may take up temporary residence over dry cultivation, small wetland areas, coastlines or surprisingly deep woodlands.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Carrascal_83-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carrascal-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Real_84-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Real-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> In winter, these eagles may occur at times at lower elevation levels and more open habitats in semi-deserts and plains, where they can appear surprisingly at home, but often prefer wetter habitats such as large river mouths, marshlands and lakes, especially where these fall in existing home range, as prey is more likely to be concentrated in such areas.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Carrascal_83-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carrascal-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Usually Bonelli's eagles live at an elevation of 1,500&#160;m (4,900&#160;ft) or lower in Europe, to 2,000&#160;m (6,600&#160;ft) in their African Atlas mountain homes and to as high an elevation 3,000&#160;m (9,800&#160;ft) in Asia and even 3,750&#160;m (12,300&#160;ft) in residence in <a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a>. The main elevation where the species resides in the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a> falls between 1,200 and 2,000&#160;m (3,900 and 6,600&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behaviour_and_dietary_ecology">Behaviour and dietary ecology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bonelli%27s_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Behaviour and dietary ecology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Raptor_in_motion_poster_(photomontage).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Raptor_in_motion_poster_%28photomontage%29.jpg/220px-Raptor_in_motion_poster_%28photomontage%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Raptor_in_motion_poster_%28photomontage%29.jpg/330px-Raptor_in_motion_poster_%28photomontage%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Raptor_in_motion_poster_%28photomontage%29.jpg/440px-Raptor_in_motion_poster_%28photomontage%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1366" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Raptor_in_motion_poster_(photomontage).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bonelli's eagles are agile, fast-flying raptors.</div></div></div>
<p>This species is very aerial, often given to soaring and circling over its home range. Like most raptorial birds, it mainly lives solitarily or in a breeding pair.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The Bonelli's eagle is a powerful predator, and has been described as rather “bold and rapacious”.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_12-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown2_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown2-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Its primary hunting methods recall those of a powerful <i><a href="/wiki/Accipiter" title="Accipiter">Accipiter</a></i> such as a <a href="/wiki/Northern_goshawk" title="Northern goshawk">goshawk</a>. Most commonly, this eagle still-hunts, often either utilizing a concealed tree perch or a lofty spot in irregular rocky terrain to watch for prey activity. Upon spotting its quarry, it often dashes out rapidly to take birds as they take off or a mammal as it runs for cover, at times making lengthy tail-chase that may continue between trees or into tree stands or bushes. Not infrequently as a latter part of a tail chase, these eagles (again reminiscent of a goshawk) will occasionally walk on the ground to obtain their prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Whistler_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whistler-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marmasse_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marmasse-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Bonelli's eagles also hunt in a quartering flying style relatively close to the ground (in a fashion reminiscent of a <a href="/wiki/Harrier_(bird)" title="Harrier (bird)">harrier</a>) or patrols hillsides for prey activity.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marmasse_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marmasse-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Bonelli's eagles will also occasionally stoop from a soaring height onto prey. Mostly, this predator takes birds from on or near the ground but some snatched them from bushes and, seldomly, water. It has been known to have sufficient agility to catch some birds from active flight.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Whistler_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whistler-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marmasse_89-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marmasse-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> In one case, a Bonelli's eagle was observed to fly below a <a href="/wiki/Western_jackdaw" title="Western jackdaw">jackdaw</a> and swoop upwards to grab it from below.<sup id="cite_ref-Marmasse_89-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marmasse-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Tandem hunting by a lifelong pair is quite common, almost exceptionally so in this species. One eagle tends to fly directly above the other, with several cases of one eagle scattering a bird flock for the other eagle to quickly single out, in a similar style to tandem-hunting <a href="/wiki/Laggar_falcon" title="Laggar falcon">laggar falcons</a> (<i>Falco jugger</i>). However, per Spanish studies, apparently tandem hunting neither improved hunting success nor were the eagles able to capture larger prey (in fact the estimated prey size by pairs was slightly lower than that taken by each mate hunting by itself) while hunting in tandem. It was hypothesized that tandem hunting is more important to the socio-sexual relations of the pair rather than capture of a significant amount of prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Watve_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watve-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Martinez_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martinez-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Compared to most other <a href="/wiki/Aquilinae" title="Aquilinae">booted eagles</a>, Bonelli's eagle takes a great majority of its prey alive and seldom comes to carrion or <a href="/wiki/Kleptoparasitism" title="Kleptoparasitism">pirates</a> foods from other raptors.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_12-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown2_34-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown2-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> However, it will readily come to previously injured prey, especially <a href="/wiki/Water_bird" title="Water bird">water birds</a> shot by <a href="/wiki/Hunting" title="Hunting">duck hunters</a>, and will readily take young animals across most prey classes.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Also, in <a href="/wiki/Keoladeo_National_Park" title="Keoladeo National Park">Keoladeo National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, Bonelli's eagles were observed to habitually follow <a href="/wiki/Harrier_(bird)" title="Harrier (bird)">harriers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clanga_(genus)" title="Clanga (genus)">spotted eagles</a> and other <i><a href="/wiki/Aquila_(genus)" title="Aquila (genus)">Aquila</a></i> eagles in order to capture <a href="/wiki/Water_bird" title="Water bird">water birds</a> incidentally flushed during their flybys.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Prakash_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prakash-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bonelli%27s_Eagle_with_prey_-_Montsonis_-_Spain_MG_4679_(24590808223).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Bonelli%27s_Eagle_with_prey_-_Montsonis_-_Spain_MG_4679_%2824590808223%29.jpg/220px-Bonelli%27s_Eagle_with_prey_-_Montsonis_-_Spain_MG_4679_%2824590808223%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Bonelli%27s_Eagle_with_prey_-_Montsonis_-_Spain_MG_4679_%2824590808223%29.jpg/330px-Bonelli%27s_Eagle_with_prey_-_Montsonis_-_Spain_MG_4679_%2824590808223%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Bonelli%27s_Eagle_with_prey_-_Montsonis_-_Spain_MG_4679_%2824590808223%29.jpg/440px-Bonelli%27s_Eagle_with_prey_-_Montsonis_-_Spain_MG_4679_%2824590808223%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1140" data-file-height="760" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bonelli%27s_Eagle_with_prey_-_Montsonis_-_Spain_MG_4679_(24590808223).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Bonelli's eagle that has caught a <a href="/wiki/Rock_pigeon" class="mw-redirect" title="Rock pigeon">rock pigeon</a>, one of the favored prey species.</div></div></div>
<p>Overall, Bonelli's eagles take a fairly wide variety of prey. Across its wide range, their prey spectrum has been known to include perhaps up to nearly 200 prey species. Dietary studies have primarily been conducted in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">western Europe</a>, though some study has gone into their food habits elsewhere (being well known in <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> and, less so, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Moleon_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moleon-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Iezekiel_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Iezekiel-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Brown &amp; Amadon (1986) considered the Bonelli's eagles prey size range as nearly as extensive as the most massive <a href="/wiki/Aquilinae" title="Aquilinae">booted eagles</a>, such as the golden eagle and the <a href="/wiki/Martial_eagle" title="Martial eagle">martial eagle</a> (<i>Polemaetus bellicosus</i>) (but mainly may have been describing the  <a href="/wiki/African_hawk-eagle" title="African hawk-eagle">African hawk-eagle</a> that was lumped at the time).<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_12-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Bonelli's eagles mainly hunts <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>, taking <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a> and other prey types on a more local and sporadic basis. In <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">western Europe</a>, it is considered something of a specialist predator on <a href="/wiki/European_rabbit" title="European rabbit">rabbits</a> and <a href="/wiki/Red-legged_partridge" title="Red-legged partridge">partridges</a>, though other birds such as <a href="/wiki/Pigeon" class="mw-redirect" title="Pigeon">pigeons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yellow-legged_gull" title="Yellow-legged gull">gulls</a> and <a href="/wiki/Corvid" class="mw-redirect" title="Corvid">corvids</a> sometimes are taken as much or more so depending on local prey population trends.<sup id="cite_ref-Moleon_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moleon-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Pellet analysis is considered the most reliable way to get a complete picture of Bonelli's eagle dietary habits.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Despite its predaceous power, typically the average size of prey taken are within average range for a raptorial bird and it may take smaller prey on average than its mildly smaller cousin, the <a href="/wiki/African_hawk-eagle" title="African hawk-eagle">African hawk-eagle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steyn_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steyn-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Morena" title="Sierra Morena">Sierra Morena</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, the mean size of prey taken was estimated at 630&#160;g (1.39&#160;lb), while in <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> the mean prey size was estimated at 877&#160;g (1.933&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Jordano_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jordano-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Alivizatos_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alivizatos-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> A subsequent study in Spain, however, posited the mean prey size as lower than in the past, stating that prey taken by males averaged an estimated 416&#160;g (14.7&#160;oz) and by females at 459&#160;g (1.012&#160;lb), probably due to increased importance of <a href="/wiki/Pigeon" class="mw-redirect" title="Pigeon">pigeons</a> and reduced numbers of <a href="/wiki/Rabbit" title="Rabbit">rabbits</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Martinez_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martinez-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Thus, on average, prey sizes average about 20-45% of the Bonelli's eagles own weight.<sup id="cite_ref-Martinez_91-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martinez-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jordano_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jordano-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Alivizatos_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alivizatos-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marti_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, the latter Spanish study found hunting success of Bonelli's eagles to average around 28.5%, a slightly higher hunting success rate than <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagles</a> (20%) or <a href="/wiki/Lesser_spotted_eagle" title="Lesser spotted eagle">lesser spotted eagles</a> (<i>Clanga pomarina</i>) (24%) but slightly lower than <a href="/wiki/Greater_spotted_eagle" title="Greater spotted eagle">greater spotted eagles</a> (<i>Clanga clanga</i>) (34%).<sup id="cite_ref-Martinez_91-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martinez-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rabbits_and_other_lagomorphs">Rabbits and other lagomorphs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bonelli%27s_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Rabbits and other lagomorphs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Katoenstaartkonijn_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Katoenstaartkonijn_1.jpg/220px-Katoenstaartkonijn_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Katoenstaartkonijn_1.jpg/330px-Katoenstaartkonijn_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Katoenstaartkonijn_1.jpg/440px-Katoenstaartkonijn_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="687" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Katoenstaartkonijn_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/European_rabbit" title="European rabbit">European rabbits</a> are strongly favored prey for Bonelli's eagles in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">western Europe</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>More than any other, the <a href="/wiki/European_rabbit" title="European rabbit">European rabbit</a> (<i>Oryctolagus cuniculus</i>) is considered the most important prey species for most European Bonelli's eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Moleon_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moleon-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In the largest European studies, the rabbit is usually the leading prey species: such as in <a href="/wiki/Catalonia" title="Catalonia">Catalonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> where rabbits comprised 22.54% of 2254 prey items (and 33.3%  of the prey biomass) and in <a href="/wiki/Provence" title="Provence">Provence</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> where rabbits made up 16.4% of 2742 prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-Mayor_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mayor-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Morvan_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morvan-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> In the third largest western European study, rabbits were secondary in number to pigeons (at 18.4% of 1641 prey items) but were still the largest contributors of biomass, at 33.2%.<sup id="cite_ref-Palma_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palma-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Even where non-native, such as the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Aegean islands">Aegean islands</a> of <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, the European rabbit dominated the foods of this eagle, comprising 40.8% by number and 46.6% by biomass of the foods.<sup id="cite_ref-Alivizatos_96-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alivizatos-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> In Spain, it was found that about three-quarters of studied floating juvenile Bonelli's eagles were hunting rabbits almost exclusively, apparently as they were easier to capture despite their larger size than bird prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Caro_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Caro-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Research determines that Bonelli's eagle are often attracted to scrub areas during hunting forays to catch sight of rabbits foraying out of vegetative cover.
