Since young juvenile and yearling rabbits are forced out to more open feeding spots by dominant adult rabbits, they are disproportionately often selected by Bonelli's eagles and other avian predators. Rabbits become more commonly caught during the summer when the young rabbits tend to disperse.<sup id="cite_ref-Martinez_91-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martinez-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Moleon_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moleon-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Palomares_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palomares-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> On the contrary, 86.2% of the rabbits taken in southwest Portugal were reportedly adults.<sup id="cite_ref-Palma_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palma-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Most rabbits caught by Bonelli's eagle were estimated to weigh between 500 and 1,500&#160;g (1.1 and 3.3&#160;lb) (from the size of a kit to a smallish adult) per Spanish studies, with an estimated average weight in Spain of 857&#160;g (1.889&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Moleon_7-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moleon-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mayor_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mayor-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> A study in southeastern Spain estimated that the region's Bonelli's eagles claim about 337 rabbits during the breeding season and 237 rabbits during non-breeding during the course of a year, so despite their heavy predation barely make a dent on the overall population of rabbits (effecting less than 2.5% of the population at peak).<sup id="cite_ref-Moleon3_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moleon3-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> The native western European population of wild rabbit has been heavily depleted by <a href="/wiki/Myxomatosis" title="Myxomatosis">myxomatosis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rabbit_haemorrhagic_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Rabbit haemorrhagic disease">rabbit haemorrhagic disease</a>, having been reduced by an estimated 50-70%. While the overall numbers seemingly taken by them reduced by as much as a third between 1968 and 2009, on evidence Bonelli's eagle still sought them out and hunted rabbits preferentially even during the non-breeding season when their numbers dip to their lowest.<sup id="cite_ref-Moleon3_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moleon3-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> In additional, significant numbers of other <a href="/wiki/Lagomorph" class="mw-redirect" title="Lagomorph">lagomorphs</a> may be taken, extending to occasional <a href="/wiki/Granada_hare" title="Granada hare">Granada hares</a> (<i>Lepus granatensis</i>) as well as accounts of Bonelli's eagles hunting <a href="/wiki/European_hare" title="European hare">European hares</a> (<i>Lepus europaeus</i>) in the Greek isles and <a href="/wiki/Indian_hare" title="Indian hare">Indian hares</a> (<i>Lepus nigricollis</i>) in the lower <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Handrinos_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Handrinos-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Miranzo_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miranzo-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gamebirds_and_pigeons">Gamebirds and pigeons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bonelli%27s_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Gamebirds and pigeons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bonellis_Eagle_kill_DSCN8964.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Bonellis_Eagle_kill_DSCN8964.jpg/220px-Bonellis_Eagle_kill_DSCN8964.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Bonellis_Eagle_kill_DSCN8964.jpg/330px-Bonellis_Eagle_kill_DSCN8964.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Bonellis_Eagle_kill_DSCN8964.jpg/440px-Bonellis_Eagle_kill_DSCN8964.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4608" data-file-height="3456" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bonellis_Eagle_kill_DSCN8964.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bonelli's eagle with a freshly caught <a href="/wiki/Grey_junglefowl" title="Grey junglefowl">grey junglefowl</a>. Gamebirds such as junglefowl are favored in the diet whenever available.</div></div></div>
<p>The main secondary wild prey species associated with Bonelli's eagles is the <a href="/wiki/Red-legged_partridge" title="Red-legged partridge">red-legged partridge</a> (<i>Alectoris rufa</i>). Although at times capable of evading the attentions of eagles, this partridge occurs in conveys in the same mixed scrub that hold rabbits and is taken whenever the eagles are lucky enough to have the element of surprise. About 383 red-legged partridges were estimated to be hunted annually in one study area of southwestern Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-Martinez_91-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martinez-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Moleon_7-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moleon-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Moleon3_103-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moleon3-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> In the large Spanish study of <a href="/wiki/Catalonia" title="Catalonia">Catalonia</a>, French study of <a href="/wiki/Provence" title="Provence">Provence</a> and in southwest <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>, the red-legged partridge made up 9.57%, 11.6% and 17.2% of the diet by number, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Mayor_98-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mayor-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Morvan_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morvan-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Palma_100-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palma-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> More so than any other prey type outside of western Europe, <a href="/wiki/Gamebird" class="mw-redirect" title="Gamebird">gamebirds</a> such as partridges seem to be globally the most favored prey type where available for Bonelli's eagle. In <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, a review of 528 prey items, revealed that the <a href="/wiki/Chukar_partridge" title="Chukar partridge">chukar</a> (<i>Alectoris chukar</i>) was the main prey at 31.4% of the diet.<sup id="cite_ref-Iezekiel_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Iezekiel-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> More than a dozen gamebirds have been detected in the foods of this species from <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> with at least a half dozen genera turning up in a few reviews of their ecology in India.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pande_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pande-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> At times, even adult <a href="/wiki/Indian_peafowl" title="Indian peafowl">Indian peafowl</a> (<i>Pavo cristatus</i>), potentially weighing up to 6&#160;kg (13&#160;lb), have been dispatched by this species.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Lesser_Sunda_Islands" title="Lesser Sunda Islands">Lesser Sunda Islands</a>, most eye-witness accounts of their hunting habits indicate that wild (or, on some islands, introduced) <a href="/wiki/Green_junglefowl" title="Green junglefowl">green junglefowl</a> (<i>Gallus varius</i>) as well as <a href="/wiki/Chicken" title="Chicken">village chickens</a> (<i>Gallus gallus</i>) are likely to be the most important prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Trainor_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Trainor-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Beyond  gamebirds, <a href="/wiki/Pigeon" class="mw-redirect" title="Pigeon">pigeons</a> are the other most significant avian prey type. The two larger European pigeons, the oft feral or domestic <a href="/wiki/Rock_pigeon" class="mw-redirect" title="Rock pigeon">rock pigeon</a> (<i>Columba livia</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/Common_wood_pigeon" title="Common wood pigeon">common wood pigeon</a> (<i>Columba palumbus</i>), are almost solely favored among this group where encountered. In southwest Portugal, pigeons have surpassed rabbits (due to their disease-based decline) to become the most important prey. Here, attempts were made to parse the proportion of <a href="/wiki/Rock_pigeon" class="mw-redirect" title="Rock pigeon">feral pigeons</a> that were taken against the number of <a href="/wiki/Domestic_pigeon" title="Domestic pigeon">domestic pigeons</a> (since <a href="/wiki/Pigeon_keeping" title="Pigeon keeping">pigeon fanciers</a> frequently persecute this eagle due its allegedly heavily predation of domestic birds). Of the 1497 prey items overall, feral pigeons were found to comprise 30.1% of the food by number and 26% of the biomass while the domestic types made up only 9.7% of the diet by number and 7.2% of the biomass.<sup id="cite_ref-Palma_100-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palma-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Catalonia" title="Catalonia">Catalonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, unidentified pigeons made up 17.8% of the foods and 17.4% of the biomass while identified common wood pigeons made up a further 6.24% of the number and 6.54% of the biomass, while a smaller study from the same area boosted wood pigeons to make up 11.3% of 524 prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-Mayor_98-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mayor-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Resano_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Resano-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, rock and common wood pigeons collectively made up 27.7% of the diet.<sup id="cite_ref-Iezekiel_93-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Iezekiel-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_birds">Other birds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bonelli%27s_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Other birds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bonelli%27s_Eagle_(Aquila_fasciata)_(38711946095).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Bonelli%27s_Eagle_%28Aquila_fasciata%29_%2838711946095%29.jpg/220px-Bonelli%27s_Eagle_%28Aquila_fasciata%29_%2838711946095%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Bonelli%27s_Eagle_%28Aquila_fasciata%29_%2838711946095%29.jpg/330px-Bonelli%27s_Eagle_%28Aquila_fasciata%29_%2838711946095%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Bonelli%27s_Eagle_%28Aquila_fasciata%29_%2838711946095%29.jpg/440px-Bonelli%27s_Eagle_%28Aquila_fasciata%29_%2838711946095%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1800" data-file-height="1335" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bonelli%27s_Eagle_(Aquila_fasciata)_(38711946095).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Bonelli's eagle being <a href="/wiki/Mobbing_(animal_behavior)" title="Mobbing (animal behavior)">mobbed</a> by <a href="/wiki/House_crow" title="House crow">house crows</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, as the eagle is a common predator of corvids.</div></div></div>
<p>Other medium-sized birds are taken widely by Bonelli's eagles. A surprisingly popular dietary choice in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">western Europe</a> was for <a href="/wiki/Yellow-legged_gull" title="Yellow-legged gull">yellow-legged gulls</a> (<i>Larus michahellis</i>), weighing an estimated 1,119&#160;g (2.467&#160;lb). In the 2724 prey items in <a href="/wiki/Provence" title="Provence">Provence</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, this gull was second only to the rabbit in number, comprising 14.6% of the diet.<sup id="cite_ref-Mayor_98-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mayor-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Morvan_99-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morvan-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Other <a href="/wiki/Gull" title="Gull">gulls</a> are readily taken by Bonelli's eagles as well as wide diversity of other <a href="/wiki/Water_bird" title="Water bird">water birds</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Rail_(bird)" title="Rail (bird)">rails</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_stone-curlew" title="Eurasian stone-curlew">stone curlews</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northern_lapwing" title="Northern lapwing">lapwings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sandpiper" title="Sandpiper">sandpipers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Procellariformes" class="mw-redirect" title="Procellariformes">tubenoses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cormorant" title="Cormorant">cormorants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heron" title="Heron">herons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Moleon_7-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moleon-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mayor_98-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mayor-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Morvan_99-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morvan-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Palma_100-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palma-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Resano2_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Resano2-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Water birds taken Bonelli's eagles may vary in size from wading birds as small as 48&#160;g (1.7&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Common_sandpiper" title="Common sandpiper">common sandpiper</a> (<i>Acitis hypoleucos</i>) and diving birds as small as 174&#160;g (6.1&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Little_grebe" title="Little grebe">little grebes</a> (<i>Tachybaptus ruficollis</i>) to those as large as adults of 3.18&#160;kg (7.0&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Painted_stork" title="Painted stork">painted storks</a> (<i>Ciconia leucocephala</i>), 3.31&#160;kg (7.3&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Greylag_goose" title="Greylag goose">greylag goose</a> (<i>Anser anser</i>) (though reportedly taken while injured by buckshot in India), and 5.5&#160;kg (12&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Common_crane" title="Common crane">common crane</a> (<i>Grus grus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CRC_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Palma_100-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palma-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Beton_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beton-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Aviles_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aviles-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Corvid" class="mw-redirect" title="Corvid">Corvids</a>, of a dozen or more species and up to the size of the 1.1&#160;kg (2.4&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Common_raven" title="Common raven">common raven</a> (<i>Corvus corax</i>), are taken in considerable numbers in differing parts of the range.<sup id="cite_ref-Moleon_7-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moleon-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Palma_100-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palma-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Provence" title="Provence">Provence</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_magpie" title="Eurasian magpie">Eurasian magpie</a> (<i>Pica pica</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Western_jackdaw" title="Western jackdaw">western jackdaw</a> (<i>Corvus monedula</i>) made up 10.17% and 9.95% of the diet respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Morvan_99-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morvan-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> In Portugal, <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_jay" title="Eurasian jay">Eurasian jay</a> comprised 7.5% by number but only 2.7% of the biomass.<sup id="cite_ref-Palma_100-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palma-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Corvids were the leading prey for Bonelli's eagles in <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>, with the Eurasian magpie comprising 12.3% of the diet (though largely young were reportedly taken) and <a href="/wiki/Carrion_crow" title="Carrion crow">carrion crows</a> (<i>Corvus corone</i>) making up a further 10.76%.<sup id="cite_ref-Abuladze_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abuladze-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Aegean islands">Aegeans</a> of Greece, carrion crows comprised 14.1% of the prey by number and 8.8% of the biomass, while south of Turkey in <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, western jackdaw comprised 7.6% of the foods.<sup id="cite_ref-Iezekiel_93-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Iezekiel-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Alivizatos_96-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alivizatos-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Other assorted avian prey groups taken in usually smaller numbers include <a href="/wiki/Cuckoo" title="Cuckoo">cuckoos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swift" title="Swift">swifts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bustard" title="Bustard">bustards</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_nightjar" title="European nightjar">nightjars</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_bee-eater" title="European bee-eater">bee-eaters</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_roller" title="European roller">rollers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_hoopoe" title="Eurasian hoopoe">hoopoes</a>. <a href="/wiki/Woodpecker" title="Woodpecker">woodpeckers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parrot" title="Parrot">parrots</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Moleon_7-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moleon-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mayor_98-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mayor-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Palma_100-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palma-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Resano2_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Resano2-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Abuladze_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abuladze-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Among passerines, which are usually quite secondary besides corvids, they've been known to hunt various <a href="/wiki/Lark" title="Lark">larks</a> (up to nearly 11% of the diet in Georgia), <a href="/wiki/Iberian_grey_shrike" title="Iberian grey shrike">shrikes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swallow" title="Swallow">swallows</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dunnock" title="Dunnock">accentors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Old_World_flycatcher" title="Old World flycatcher">Old World flycatchers</a> (at least 10 different species), <a href="/wiki/Thrush_(bird)" title="Thrush (bird)">thrushes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meadow_pipit" title="Meadow pipit">pipits</a>, <a href="/wiki/Starling" title="Starling">starlings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emberiza" class="mw-redirect" title="Emberiza">buntings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finch" title="Finch">finches</a> and <a href="/wiki/House_sparrow" title="House sparrow">Old world sparrows</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Moleon_7-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moleon-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vittorio_86-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vittorio-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mayor_98-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mayor-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Morvan_99-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morvan-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Alivizatos_96-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alivizatos-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Palma_100-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palma-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Resano2_109-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Resano2-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Beton_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beton-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Abuladze_113-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abuladze-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gil-Sanchez_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gil-Sanchez-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kumawat_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kumawat-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Elosegui_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elosegui-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> In total, some 130 bird species may be taken and birds as a whole almost always form the most ample part of the diet compared to other classes: 69.5% and 80.97% of the biomass in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_France" title="Southern France">south of France</a>, 67.7% in <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a> and 62.6% in <a href="/wiki/Catalonia" title="Catalonia">Catalonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Moleon_7-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moleon-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mayor_98-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mayor-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Morvan_99-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morvan-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Resano_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Resano-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Resano2_109-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Resano2-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Abuladze_113-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abuladze-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_assorted_prey">Other assorted prey</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bonelli%27s_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Other assorted prey">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Beyond the high significance of rabbits (and sometimes other lagomorphs), other mammals are rarely as important or diverse in the diet of Bonelli's eagles as birds are. A couple of rodents can be locally significant secondary prey, however. The <a href="/wiki/Red_squirrel" title="Red squirrel">red squirrel</a> (<i>Sciurus vulgaris</i>), with a mean estimated mass in Spain of 241&#160;g (8.5&#160;oz), was reported in almost all <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">western Europe</a> studies, with about 130 reported as taken in studies from <a href="/wiki/Provence" title="Provence">Provence</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mayor_98-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mayor-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Morvan_99-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morvan-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Black_rat" title="Black rat">black rat</a> (<i>Rattus rattus</i>), of similar size to the squirrel at an average of about 200&#160;g (7.1&#160;oz) was an important secondary food source in islands south and east of <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, being the second most common prey species in <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> (15.5% of 528 prey items) and fifth most important prey species in the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Aegean islands">Aegean islands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Iezekiel_93-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Iezekiel-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Alivizatos_96-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alivizatos-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Northwestern_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Northwestern Africa">northwestern Africa</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, it was reported that the <a href="/wiki/Fat_sand_rat" title="Fat sand rat">fat sand rat</a> (<i>Psammomys obesus</i>), another rodent of similar size, was amongst the favorite foods locally for Bonelli's eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Other rodent species known in the diet of Bonelli's eagles have included other <a href="/wiki/Squirrel" title="Squirrel">squirrels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Common_gundi" title="Common gundi">gundis</a>, assorted <a href="/wiki/Mice" class="mw-redirect" title="Mice">mice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">voles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dormice" class="mw-redirect" title="Dormice">dormice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blind_mole_rat" class="mw-redirect" title="Blind mole rat">blind mole rats</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Moleon_7-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moleon-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Morvan_99-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morvan-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Burger_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burger-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Beyond a few species of <a href="/wiki/Hedgehog" title="Hedgehog">hedgehogs</a>, additional mammalian prey for this species, although seldom taken, can be relatively large. They've been known to attack the young of various ungulates include <a href="/wiki/Blackbuck" title="Blackbuck">blackbuck</a> (<i>Antilope cervicapra</i>), <a href="/wiki/Chinkara" title="Chinkara">chinkara</a> (<i>Gazella bennettii</i>), <a href="/wiki/Goat" title="Goat">domestic goats</a> (<i>Capra aegagrus hircus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Domestic_sheep" class="mw-redirect" title="Domestic sheep">domestic sheep</a> (<i>Ovis aries</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Moleon_7-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moleon-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Aegean islands">Aegean islands</a>, live-caught but often young and small goat kids comprised 8.5% of the foods and 24.3% of the biomass at nests.<sup id="cite_ref-Alivizatos_96-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alivizatos-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Among <a href="/wiki/Carnivora" title="Carnivora">carnivorans</a>, Bonelli's eagles have reportedly attacked <a href="/wiki/Red_fox" title="Red fox">red fox</a> (<i>Vulpes vulpes</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Wildcat" title="Wildcat">wildcats</a> (<i>Felis silvestris</i>) (probably mostly kits and kittens of these two species) in western Europe as well as <a href="/wiki/Stone_marten" class="mw-redirect" title="Stone marten">stone martens</a> (<i>Martes foina</i>) and assorted <a href="/wiki/Weasel" title="Weasel">weasels</a>. Meanwhile, adult <a href="/wiki/Bengal_fox" title="Bengal fox">Bengal fox</a> (<i>Vulpes bengalensis</i>) have reportedly been caught in India.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Moleon_7-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moleon-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> In France and Spain, mammals overall comprised 34.8% and 26.1% of the diet, respectively, whereas in Georgia they made up 15.4% of the  diet.<sup id="cite_ref-Morvan_99-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morvan-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Resano_108-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Resano-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Resano2_109-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Resano2-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Abuladze_113-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abuladze-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">Reptiles</a> are usually secondary prey throughout the range. Though they are known to hunt <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">snakes</a>, Bonelli's eagles rarely hunt them and generally seem to pursue <a href="/wiki/Lizard" title="Lizard">lizards</a> by preference. In Cyprus, <a href="/wiki/Stellagama" title="Stellagama">starred agamas</a> (<i>Stellagama stellio</i>) comprised 5.9% of the food, unidentified <i><a href="/wiki/Lacerta_(genus)" title="Lacerta (genus)">Lacerta</a></i> lizards 10.76% of diet in <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a> (and reptiles altogether adding up to 16.9% of the food by number), and <a href="/wiki/Ocellated_lizard" title="Ocellated lizard">ocellated lizard</a> (<i>Timon lepidus</i>), at 228&#160;g (8.0&#160;oz) in mean body mass, 3.97% biomass and 7.05% by number in <a href="/wiki/Catalonia" title="Catalonia">Catalonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mayor_98-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mayor-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Iezekiel_93-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Iezekiel-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Abuladze_113-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abuladze-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Desert_monitor" title="Desert monitor">Desert monitor</a> (<i>Varanus griseus</i>) and probably assorted other monitor lizards were reportedly amongst the leading prey for Bonelli's eagles in several parts of India.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pande_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pande-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kumawat_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kumawat-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Minor prey includes <a href="/wiki/Common_toad" title="Common toad">toads</a> and possibly a few other types of <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibian</a>. Potentially <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a> and/or other <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a> may be taken but these may incidentally consumed (i.e. undigested food from the stomachs of prey).<sup id="cite_ref-Morvan_99-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morvan-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Burger_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burger-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interspecies_predatory_relationships">Interspecies predatory relationships</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bonelli%27s_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Interspecies predatory relationships">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bonellis_Eagle_(Adult_%26_Juv)-3732.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Bonellis_Eagle_%28Adult_%26_Juv%29-3732.jpg/220px-Bonellis_Eagle_%28Adult_%26_Juv%29-3732.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Bonellis_Eagle_%28Adult_%26_Juv%29-3732.jpg/330px-Bonellis_Eagle_%28Adult_%26_Juv%29-3732.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Bonellis_Eagle_%28Adult_%26_Juv%29-3732.jpg/440px-Bonellis_Eagle_%28Adult_%26_Juv%29-3732.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3500" data-file-height="2625" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bonellis_Eagle_(Adult_%26_Juv)-3732.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>While other birds of prey present some competition for Bonelli's eagles, intraspecific conflicts are the most significant. Here an adult escorts out a wandering juvenile from its range.</div></div></div>
<p>Bonelli's eagles frequently occur in range with multiple other eagles and competing predators. Almost certainly the most direct competitor from their European range to the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> is their much larger cousin, the <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagle</a>. Habitat preferences overlap between these two eagles with both species favoring rocky habitats, though the golden eagle regularly dwells at slightly higher elevations with <a href="/wiki/Alpine_tundra" title="Alpine tundra">alpine meadows</a> (though is as adaptive to low elevations as the Bonelli's so long as habitat is favorable and undisturbed).<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jordano_95-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jordano-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Competition between the eagles has been reported in particular from Spain, in areas such as <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Morena" title="Sierra Morena">Sierra Morena</a>. Both species excluded each other from mutual territories and had broadly overlapping food habits. However, the Bonelli's eagle took more birds and golden eagle more singularly hunted rabbits. Mean distance between nests on a plot of 2,200&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (850&#160;sq&#160;mi) was found to be 10.2&#160;km (6.3&#160;mi) for 8 pairs of golden eagles and 11.4&#160;km (7.1&#160;mi) for 10 pairs of Bonelli's.  The two can co-exist with sufficiently large ranges as long as they are able to maintain their own range, with the existence of trophic segregation (by size and the more avian based diet of the Bonelli's) and the lag in the breeding periods, as these natural mechanisms would allow the coexistence of both species in the mountain.<sup id="cite_ref-Jordano_95-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jordano-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Moreno-Rueda_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moreno-Rueda-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Cases of golden eagles taking over prior Bonelli's eagles territories have been reported but usually golden eagles only takes up the prior Bonelli's territory when the latter vanishes due to unrelated (often <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">anthropogenic</a>) causes not direct competition or usurpation. A minor negative effect has been probably correlated with golden eagles not infrequently attacking and displacing juvenile and subadult Bonelli's eagles and can tend to be behaviorally dominant in keeping with its larger size. This in turn presumably hampers the ability of the Bonelli's to expand their range after declines and stabilize their population.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Further east, in <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, Bonelli's and golden eagles are competitors as well. In the dry, barren <a href="/wiki/Negev_desert" class="mw-redirect" title="Negev desert">Negev desert</a>, golden eagles nests were found 13.1&#160;km (8.1&#160;mi) apart and Bonelli's were scarce. In the <a href="/wiki/Judean_desert" class="mw-redirect" title="Judean desert">Judean desert</a>, which has more annual rainfall and more available prey, the distance between golden eagle nests averaged 16&#160;km (9.9&#160;mi) and the Bonelli's eagle easily outnumbered them. Apparently, the Bonelli's eagle exceptionally outcompeted its larger cousin here due to a subtle topographic variation in the habitat.<sup id="cite_ref-Bahat_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bahat-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> In Spain, Bonelli's eagles share cliff habitats beyond golden eagles also with <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">peregrine falcons</a> (<i>Falco peregrinus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Common_raven" title="Common raven">common ravens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_eagle-owl" title="Eurasian eagle-owl">Eurasian eagle-owls</a> (<i>Bubo bubo</i>) and three species of <a href="/wiki/Old_World_vulture" title="Old World vulture">vulture</a>. The eagles tend to dominate the smaller carnivorous birds in most circumstances, even the swifter peregrine.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> However, the still larger <a href="/wiki/Griffon_vulture" title="Griffon vulture">griffon vulture</a> (<i>Gyps fulvus</i>) was apparently a routine territory and nest usurper of other birds of prey, displacing golden eagles, <a href="/wiki/Bearded_vulture" title="Bearded vulture">bearded vultures</a> (<i>Gypaetus barbatus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_vulture" title="Egyptian vulture">Egyptian vultures</a> (<i>Neophron percnopterus</i>) from their nests as well as 9 out of 23 eyries built by Bonelli's eagles in the study area.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Despite their prior claimed “dominance” over the swift falcons, at least three cases have been observed of peregrine falcons usurping Bonelli's eagle (presumably through routine harassment and dive-bombing) nests in Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-Plugeuzuelos_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Plugeuzuelos-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Beyond golden eagles, peregrines and griffon vultures, <a href="/wiki/Tawny_owl" title="Tawny owl">tawny owls</a> (<i>Strix alucco</i>) have been known to take over old Bonelli's eagle nests.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>European rabbits have a huge range of predators in the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Iberian peninsula">Iberian peninsula</a>, with at least 30 different species known to hunt the once densely-populated lagomorph.<sup id="cite_ref-Moleon_7-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moleon-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Moleon3_103-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moleon3-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Palomares_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palomares-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Besides the overlapping ranges of the Bonelli's and golden eagles, most other birds of prey that hunt rabbits extensively are partitioned from the potential depletive effect of competition by differences in habitat preferences, hunting techniques and temporal activity. Beyond the specialized mammalian predator, the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_lynx" title="Iberian lynx">Iberian lynx</a> (<i>Lynx pardinus</i>), some of the other most specialized predators of wild rabbits are Bonelli's eagles, golden eagles, <a href="/wiki/Spanish_imperial_eagle" title="Spanish imperial eagle">Spanish imperial eagles</a> (<i>Aquila adalberti</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_eagle-owl" title="Eurasian eagle-owl">Eurasian eagle-owls</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Moleon_7-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moleon-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Moleon3_103-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moleon3-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Palomares_102-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palomares-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lloveras_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lloveras-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> A comparative study indicated that the golden eagle diet was comprised 40% by rabbits, while they made up 49% for eagle-owls, 50% for Spanish imperial eagles and 61% for Bonelli's eagle.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Elsewhere, higher import has been applied for rabbits in the local diet of golden eagles as well as for Spanish imperial eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Jordano_95-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jordano-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> The mean size of rabbits taken increases more or less with the size of the avian predator: 662&#160;g (1.459&#160;lb) for <a href="/wiki/Northern_goshawk" title="Northern goshawk">northern goshawks</a>, 857&#160;g (1.889&#160;lb) for Bonelli's eagles, 1,000&#160;g (2.2&#160;lb) for <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_eagle-owl" title="Eurasian eagle-owl">Eurasian eagle-owls</a> and 1,360&#160;g (3.00&#160;lb) for golden eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jordano_95-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jordano-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Donázar,_J._1989_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Donázar,_J._1989-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Along with northern goshawks, golden eagles and Eurasian eagle-owls, Bonelli's eagle is considered a “super predator” in the European region due to its habit of hunting other predators. In contrast to the other birds of prey, they are somewhat less commonly at high predator status compared to goshawks (most common predator of other diurnal raptors in studies), golden eagles (most common predator of <a href="/wiki/Mesopredator" title="Mesopredator">mesopredator</a> mammals), and eagle-owl (most common predator of other owls).<sup id="cite_ref-Lourenco_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lourenco-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> However, they are relatively common predators of other diurnal birds of prey, per overall analysis they took such prey somewhat more regularly than did golden eagles in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Lourenco_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lourenco-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> Among the other accipitrids that the Bonelli's eagle have been known to hunt include the <a href="/wiki/Indian_spotted_eagle" title="Indian spotted eagle">Indian spotted eagle</a> (<i>Clanga hastata</i>), <a href="/wiki/European_honey_buzzard" title="European honey buzzard">European honey buzzard</a> (<i>Pernis apivorus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Red_kite" title="Red kite">red kite</a> (<i>Milvus milvus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Black_kite" title="Black kite">black kite</a> (<i>Milvus migrans</i>), <a href="/wiki/Western_marsh_harrier" title="Western marsh harrier">western marsh harrier</a> (<i>Circus aeruginosus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Montagu%27s_harrier" title="Montagu&#39;s harrier">Montagu's harrier</a> (<i>Circus pygargus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Hen_harrier" title="Hen harrier">hen harrier</a> (<i>Circus cyaenus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_sparrowhawk" title="Eurasian sparrowhawk">Eurasian sparrowhawk</a> (<i>Accipiter nisus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Shikra" title="Shikra">shikra</a> (<i>Accipiter badius</i>), <a href="/wiki/Northern_goshawk" title="Northern goshawk">northern goshawk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Long-legged_buzzard" title="Long-legged buzzard">long-legged buzzard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Common_buzzard" title="Common buzzard">common buzzard</a> (<i>Buteo buteo</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Moleon_7-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moleon-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_12-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Resano_108-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Resano-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Resano2_109-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Resano2-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gil-Sanchez_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gil-Sanchez-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Among falcons, they've been known to prey upon <a href="/wiki/Common_kestrel" title="Common kestrel">common kestrel</a> (<i>Falco tinnunculus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Lesser_kestrel" title="Lesser kestrel">lesser kestrel</a> (<i>Falco naumanni</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">peregrine falcon</a> and as for owls, <a href="/wiki/Tawny_owl" title="Tawny owl">tawny owl</a> (<i>Strix alucco</i>), <a href="/wiki/Little_owl" title="Little owl">little owl</a> (<i>Athene noctua</i>), <a href="/wiki/Long-eared_owl" title="Long-eared owl">long-eared owl</a> (<i>Asio otus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Short-eared_owl" title="Short-eared owl">short-eared owl</a> (<i>Asio flammeus</i>) and most impressively of all, in at least one instance, an adult <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_eagle-owl" title="Eurasian eagle-owl">Eurasian eagle-owl</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Moleon_7-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moleon-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vittorio_86-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vittorio-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mayor_98-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mayor-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Resano2_109-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Resano2-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Although usually classed as an <a href="/wiki/Apex_predator" title="Apex predator">apex predator</a>, as in most cases of apex predators in competitive environments, Bonelli's eagles sometimes infrequently fall victim to interspecific killings and predation as well.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_eagle-owl" title="Eurasian eagle-owl">Eurasian eagle-owls</a> have been known to prey on Bonelli's nestlings a few times and possibly also an adult at least once.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> In one case, a subadult male golden eagle preyed upon an adult male Bonelli's eagle.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stone_marten" class="mw-redirect" title="Stone marten">Stone martens</a> are also counted amongst the predators of nests (exclusively as egg thieves) in Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bonelli%27s_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Breeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bonelli%27s_eagle_(Aquila_fasciata)_mating_composite.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Bonelli%27s_eagle_%28Aquila_fasciata%29_mating_composite.jpg/220px-Bonelli%27s_eagle_%28Aquila_fasciata%29_mating_composite.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="82" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Bonelli%27s_eagle_%28Aquila_fasciata%29_mating_composite.jpg/330px-Bonelli%27s_eagle_%28Aquila_fasciata%29_mating_composite.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Bonelli%27s_eagle_%28Aquila_fasciata%29_mating_composite.jpg/440px-Bonelli%27s_eagle_%28Aquila_fasciata%29_mating_composite.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5348" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bonelli%27s_eagle_(Aquila_fasciata)_mating_composite.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>mating on the Southern bank of the <a href="/wiki/Chambal_River" title="Chambal River">Chambal River</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh" title="Uttar Pradesh">Uttar Pradesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pair_formation_and_nest_distribution">Pair formation and nest distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bonelli%27s_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Pair formation and nest distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bonelli's eagles, like most but not all raptorial birds, generally lives solitarily or in pairs. They usually mate for life.<sup id="cite_ref-Wilheim_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wilheim-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Territories are maintained through aerial displays which often involve calling, single or mutual high circling and, most frequently, sky-dancing in the area of eyrie. During this species’ sky-dances, one or other of the eagle pair plunges headlong from a great height, with its wings almost closed, before checking and rising again on stiff wings, circling to regain original altitude and diving again. The sky-dance sequence may be repeated up to 5-10 times. Occasionally but usually infrequently, territorial exclusions escalate into talon grappling between a territorial bird and an intruder.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> Aerial display extend with diminished frequency into the incubation and early nestling periods.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> In Spain, the average estimated size of a pairs home range was a very large 44.2&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (17.1&#160;sq&#160;mi), though only 27.3% of their home ranges on average were used in all seasons.<sup id="cite_ref-Perez-Garcia_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Perez-Garcia-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Banda_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Banda-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> Home ranges in <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> were estimated to average up to 130&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (50&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferreira_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferreira-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> On <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, the mean nearest neighbor distance was 7.4&#160;km (4.6&#160;mi) with 0.52-0.65 pairs per 100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Kassinis_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kassinis-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Contrary to many other raptor species,  it was found that were no significant relationship between the density of their main prey species and the distance of the neighbouring pairs.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> A dead or missing mate may be quickly replaced and mature adults have additionally been seen breeding with subadults.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bonelli%27s_Eagle_Male_and_Female.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Bonelli%27s_Eagle_Male_and_Female.jpg/220px-Bonelli%27s_Eagle_Male_and_Female.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Bonelli%27s_Eagle_Male_and_Female.jpg/330px-Bonelli%27s_Eagle_Male_and_Female.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Bonelli%27s_Eagle_Male_and_Female.jpg/440px-Bonelli%27s_Eagle_Male_and_Female.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bonelli%27s_Eagle_Male_and_Female.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bonelli's Eagle Male and Female Pair.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nests">Nests</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bonelli%27s_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Nests">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The breeding season of Bonelli's eagles is from late January/February to July in the western part of the range and November–August/September (peaking December–May) in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a>. Both members of the pair may dwell around the eyrie for up to 2–3 months before breeding. Their nest is a huge structure of branches and sticks, scarcely smaller than those built by eagles twice the size of this species, though rarely as great in depth as some old nests of the larger eagles. Often the nest completely obscures the sitting female from view unless it is seen at the same level or higher than the nest. Nest size can average up to 1.8&#160;m (5.9&#160;ft) across and 60&#160;cm (24&#160;in) deep but with repeated use the nest can range up to 2&#160;m (6.6&#160;ft) in both directions (record sized nest in India was 2.4&#160;m (7.9&#160;ft) in height). Tree nests tend to average larger than those placed on cliff ledges.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_12-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> One nest in the <a href="/wiki/Gir_National_Park" title="Gir National Park">Gir forest</a> was used periodically used over the course of 30 years.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> While they may line their active nest with greenery, it is less frequent and sparser where present than in many other birds of prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Nesting locales are often high on cliff ledge or at 5 to 40&#160;m (16 to 131&#160;ft) (usually over 10&#160;m (33&#160;ft)) above the ground in large tree.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Very rarely, nests may too be on the perimeter of buildings.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> The trees selected are often the tallest and/or most densely foliaged in a given stand.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_12-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Their close cousin, the <a href="/wiki/African_hawk-eagle" title="African hawk-eagle">African hawk-eagle</a>, usually nests on trees and rarely utilizes crags and alternate nesting sites as does the Bonelli's.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_12-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Historically, throughout their range in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">western Europe</a>, Bonelli's eagles were considered almost obligate cliff nesters on almost any rocky environment, from precipitous mountain ranges, <a href="/wiki/Canyon" title="Canyon">canyons</a> over river valleys, even down to low rocky rubble to sea cliffs.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> However, up to 52 tree nests for the species have now been recorded in southwest <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>. Often the Portuguese nesting eagles used invasive <a href="/wiki/Eucalyptus_globulus" title="Eucalyptus globulus">Tasmanian blue gum</a> (<i>Eucalyptus globulus</i>) (44.2% of the time) while a further 21.2% were on <a href="/wiki/Quercus_suber" title="Quercus suber">cork oak</a> (<i>Quercus suber</i>)  not to mention some that were placed on large shrubs, i.e. <a href="/wiki/Arbutus_unedo" title="Arbutus unedo">strawberry trees</a> (<i>Arbutus unedo</i>). The mean height of Portuguese tree nest was 23.9&#160;m (78&#160;ft). The Portuguese study further found 67.3% of the tree nests to be on hill slopes and 4.5&#160;m (15&#160;ft) average height for lowest branch, both presumably as anti-predator measure.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferreira_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferreira-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> By 2017, the expansion to using tree nests had bolstered the southwest Portugal population considerably.<sup id="cite_ref-Dias_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dias-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> In the 1990s, the first ever tree nest was found in the relative Bonelli's eagle stronghold of <a href="/wiki/Catalonia" title="Catalonia">Catalonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, while another singular tree nest was also found in the south of France.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> In India, Bonelli's eagles seem to readily switch between tree and cliff nests. The eagles of areas such as <a href="/wiki/Maharashtra" title="Maharashtra">Maharashtra</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Western_Ghats" title="Western Ghats">Western Ghats</a> are usually partial to nesting in trees while in the <a href="/wiki/Deccan_Peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Deccan Peninsula">Deccan Peninsula</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indo-Gangetic_plain" class="mw-redirect" title="Indo-Gangetic plain">Indo-Gangetic plain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Himalaya" class="mw-redirect" title="Himalaya">Himalayan foothills</a>, the eagles alternated between nesting on cliffs and lofty trees including <a href="/wiki/Bombax_ceiba" title="Bombax ceiba">red silk cotton</a> (<i>Bombax ceiba</i>), <a href="/wiki/Ficus_religiosa" title="Ficus religiosa">sacred fig</a> (<i>Ficus religiosa</i>), <a href="/wiki/Syzygium_cumini" title="Syzygium cumini">Javan plum</a> (<i>Syzygium cumini</i>) or <i><a href="/wiki/Dalbergia" title="Dalbergia">Dalbergia</a> ssp</i>. Bonelli's eagles in India may also nest close to human habitations if disturbance is low, such as in <a href="/wiki/Saurashtra_(region)" title="Saurashtra (region)">Saurashtra</a> and in Himalayan foothills, in the latter often in large <a href="/wiki/Pinus_roxburghii" title="Pinus roxburghii">chir pine</a> (<i>Pinus roxburghii</i>) near villages. In Pakistan, the species has been known to use seaside cliffs. Also, in the Indian desert zone, Bonelli's eagles are known to use relatively low rocky hills with shallow gradients, making the nest ledges relatively easy to access.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> On <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, 70% of nests were in <a href="/wiki/Pinus_brutia" title="Pinus brutia">Turkish pines</a> (<i>Pinus brutia</i>) at a mean elevation of 625&#160;m (2,051&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-Kassinis_58-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kassinis-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Often this eagle uses the same nest in successive years but also alternate nest may be used. Often somewhere between 1 and 5 nests may be built by the species on their home range. Like other birds of prey, the presence of alternate nest may be a strategy to cope with <a href="/wiki/Ectoparasitic_infestation" title="Ectoparasitic infestation">ectoparasitic infestations</a> within the nest.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_12-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Plugeuzuelos_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Plugeuzuelos-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> The construction of a new nest takes roughly a month's time.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_12-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hieraaetus_fasciatus_MWNH_0848.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Hieraaetus_fasciatus_MWNH_0848.JPG/220px-Hieraaetus_fasciatus_MWNH_0848.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Hieraaetus_fasciatus_MWNH_0848.JPG/330px-Hieraaetus_fasciatus_MWNH_0848.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Hieraaetus_fasciatus_MWNH_0848.JPG/440px-Hieraaetus_fasciatus_MWNH_0848.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3234" data-file-height="2233" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hieraaetus_fasciatus_MWNH_0848.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Bonelli's eagle's egg, from the collection <a href="/wiki/Museum_Wiesbaden" title="Museum Wiesbaden">Museum Wiesbaden</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_fasciata_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.81.2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Aquila_fasciata_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.81.2.jpg/220px-Aquila_fasciata_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.81.2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Aquila_fasciata_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.81.2.jpg/330px-Aquila_fasciata_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.81.2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Aquila_fasciata_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.81.2.jpg/440px-Aquila_fasciata_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.81.2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5197" data-file-height="3307" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_fasciata_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.81.2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Aquila fasciata</i> - <a href="/wiki/MHNT" class="mw-redirect" title="MHNT">MHNT</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_of_young">Development of young</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bonelli%27s_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Development of young">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The clutch size is usually 2, though one egg is not infrequently laid. This species seldom lays three eggs, though there are now a few records of this and even three large eaglets recorded in a nest.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> Their eggs are largely white but are often sparingly spotted and streaked with brown. In a sample of 120, egg height was measured as 62 to 76.5&#160;mm (2.44 to 3.01&#160;in), with an average of 69&#160;mm (2.7&#160;in) by 48 to 57.3&#160;mm (1.89 to 2.26&#160;in) in diameter, with an average of 54&#160;mm (2.1&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_12-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Egg laying dates peak from February to April in France, January in North Africa while in India, the peak may be December to April, sometimes even into May (as in the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_12-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Incubation lasts for 37 to 41 days in Europe but is estimated at a more prolonged 40–45 days in the more tropical <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Incubation is mainly done by the female (about 90% of the time) while males mainly capture food.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_12-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Upon hatching, the eaglets are <a href="/wiki/Altriciality" title="Altriciality">altricial</a> initially. The first feathers start appearing through the white down at 25–35 days and practically cover the body by 45 days. By the latter stage, the eaglets can normally feed themselves but may be variable in learning ability. Fledgling period is at 56 to 65 days of age (rarely as late as 70 days).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The average age at fledging in Spain was estimated at 63 days.<sup id="cite_ref-Codina_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Codina-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> Female broods about 90% of the time for first two weeks after first hatching but this decreases to 50% by the end of those weeks. The female attacks potential predators that come near the nest including other raptors. In the Indian subcontinent, they've been seen to escort <a href="/wiki/Oriental_honey_buzzard" class="mw-redirect" title="Oriental honey buzzard">oriental honey buzzards</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crested_serpent_eagle" title="Crested serpent eagle">crested serpent eagles</a> (<i>Spilornis cheela</i>), <a href="/wiki/Bearded_vulture" title="Bearded vulture">bearded vultures</a>, as well as <i><a href="/wiki/Gyps" title="Gyps">Gyps</a></i> vultures, conspecifics and corvids away from the eyrie vicinity while the presence of  <a href="/wiki/Northern_plains_gray_langur" title="Northern plains gray langur">northern plains gray langurs</a> (<i>Semnopithecus entellus</i>) was observed to provoke a fierce defensive attack. However, unlike <a href="/wiki/African_hawk-eagle" title="African hawk-eagle">African hawk-eagles</a>, Bonelli's eagles rarely attack humans at the nest.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Males at times have been observed to take a share of the brooding and rarely even feeding the eaglet(s).<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_12-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cache_(biology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cache (biology)">Caches</a> of food are often stored early on but are quickly depleted and rarely still present latter in the nesting period when the eaglets growth accelerates.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> The female lingers near nest even after brooding stage. However, the female also tends to take part in prey capture relatively early in fledging period compared to many other eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_12-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> In the latter third of the post-fledging period, the parents are rarely in attendance except when bringing food. The dependence of the young eagles may extend for about 8 to 11 weeks, but has individually varied from 50 to over 120 days. Research on conditions and habitat composition has indicated variation in the post-fledgling dependence stage is derived from habitat quality or from parental skill. However, fledgling body condition seemed to play no major role in this stage. On evidence, the young eagles drift from their parents care independently.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_12-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Codina_166-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Codina-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_fasciata_on_nest.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Aquila_fasciata_on_nest.jpg/220px-Aquila_fasciata_on_nest.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Aquila_fasciata_on_nest.jpg/330px-Aquila_fasciata_on_nest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Aquila_fasciata_on_nest.jpg/440px-Aquila_fasciata_on_nest.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="698" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_fasciata_on_nest.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fully fledged juvenile eagles lingering in the vicinity of their nest.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-Breeding">Post-Breeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bonelli%27s_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Post-Breeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One of the most significant portions of the Bonelli's eagles lifecycle is the dispersal stage. The dispersal and post-dispersal stage has been studied at length in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">western Europe</a>, with a surprising amount of individual variation being found. Here, dispersal occurred at an average age of 142 days (occasionally up to 163 days old) with a varying distance of dispersal from nest to settlement area were from 50 to 536&#160;km (31 to 333&#160;mi). The average distance of dispersal in France was 158&#160;km (98&#160;mi). Anywhere from 58% of 47 to 87% of 7 juveniles survived per these radio-tagged studies.<sup id="cite_ref-Cadahia_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cadahia-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cadahia_171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cadahia-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cheylan_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cheylan-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> The high distance dispersal of the juvenile Bonelli's eagles may potentially benefit gene flow.<sup id="cite_ref-Cadahia2_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cadahia2-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cadahia3_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cadahia3-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cugnasse2_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cugnasse2-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> At least 20 communal roosts for post-dispersal juvenile Bonelli's eagles were found in Spain. Each were found to house between 2 and 11 eagles of the species, with mean of 5.1. It was also found the juveniles were usually sharing many of the roosts with <a href="/wiki/Spanish_imperial_eagle" title="Spanish imperial eagle">Spanish imperial eagle</a> juveniles as well (in 91.4% of roost) though each species clustered separately in different parts of the trees or bushes. More infrequently, assorted other species of raptor would join the juvenile eagle roosts at dusk.<sup id="cite_ref-Communal_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Communal-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding_success_and_causes_of_failures">Breeding success and causes of failures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bonelli%27s_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Breeding success and causes of failures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_fasciata.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Aquila_fasciata.JPG/220px-Aquila_fasciata.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Aquila_fasciata.JPG/330px-Aquila_fasciata.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Aquila_fasciata.JPG/440px-Aquila_fasciata.JPG 2x" data-file-width="6000" data-file-height="4000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_fasciata.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bonelli's eagle feeding its eaglet with a partridge.</div></div></div>
<p>The breeding success of Bonelli's eagles may vary considerably. Mean fledgling success on <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> was found to be 1.44 per pair.<sup id="cite_ref-Kassinis_58-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kassinis-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>, breeding success was found to vary 0.67 (in the 1990s) to 1.37 (in the 2000s after some protection) and productivity of successful pairs was from 1.42 (2000s) to 1.51 (1990s).<sup id="cite_ref-Mascara_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mascara-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vittorio2_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vittorio2-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Of 1506 breeding attempts in western Europe, 65.7% were successful. Of these successful ones, 39.8% produced one fledgling, 59.7% produced two and only 0.5% produced three fledglings.<sup id="cite_ref-Hernandez-Matias_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hernandez-Matias-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rico_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rico-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sanchez_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sanchez-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> Like many birds of prey, <a href="/wiki/Siblicide" title="Siblicide">siblicide</a> or cainism has occurred, wherein the eldest nestling repeatedly attacks, often killing and occasionally eating their younger siblings. In about 20% of nest, the second chick survives, therefore this species is classed as a <a href="/wiki/Siblicide" title="Siblicide">facultative cainist</a> rather than an obligate one. On evidence, egg laying and hatching may grow more asynchronous when frequently interrelated outside stressors such as food supply, habitat disturbance and poor weather are applied, all of which may increase the likelihood of cainism.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_12-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hernandez-Matias_180-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hernandez-Matias-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> Whether the young have died by siblicide or via other means, Bonelli's eagles have been known to consume their own dead nestlings on a couple of occasions.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> On evidence, the younger eaglets of Bonelli's eagles and other species in areas where threatened may too survive by human intervention, wherein they remove the chicks and either raise them in semi-captivity or introduce them to a new set of parents.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> In India, habitat and the resulting prey composition were found to be the most significant drivers of breeding success. In protected areas such as <a href="/wiki/Ranthambore_National_Park" title="Ranthambore National Park">Ranthambore National Park</a>, nest often produce two fledglings, while in degraded areas such as the <a href="/wiki/Kumaun_division" class="mw-redirect" title="Kumaun division">Kumaun division</a>, they often produce just one. Fledgling number here was thought to be driven primarily by prey carrying capacity of a given area.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> When an almost fledged young was stolen by village children in India, 15 hours later, researchers introduced another which was accepted by parents. In a similar case, another Indian pair rejected its own nearly fledged eaglet after it had been stolen, however with repeated attempts was accepted and successfully fledged.<sup id="cite_ref-Pande2004_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pande2004-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> When poachers stole some eagles in Spain, a couple of pairs were found to successfully lay replacement clutches (each with the typical 2 eggs) some 25–30 days later.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">western European</a> review of 1052 breeding attempts indicated a negative correlation with colder temperatures and heavier rains during nesting. Therefore, in more temperate areas such as northern Spain, the average breeding success was lower and the young often dispersed southwards due to the cooler climate.<sup id="cite_ref-Ontiveros2_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ontiveros2-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation_and_rehabilitation">Conservation and rehabilitation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bonelli%27s_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Conservation and rehabilitation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
