<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Habichtsadler.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Habichtsadler.jpg/220px-Habichtsadler.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="296" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Habichtsadler.jpg/330px-Habichtsadler.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Habichtsadler.jpg/440px-Habichtsadler.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1102" data-file-height="1481" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Habichtsadler.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artwork from a 19th-century German Natural History book</div></div></div>
<p>Bonelli's eagles have sharply decline, at least locally inexorably and drastically, in much of their range. In the 1990s, it was estimated that the entire west <a href="/wiki/Palearctic" class="mw-redirect" title="Palearctic">Palearctic</a> held about 2000-3000 pairs with the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Iberian peninsula">Iberian peninsula</a> (750-845 pairs) and <a href="/wiki/Northwestern_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Northwestern Africa">northwestern Africa</a> (1000 or so) being the core areas. In the mid-1990s, it was indicated that there were 938-1039 pairs in all of Europe, about 75-80% of which in Spain with an estimated 75-90 in <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>, 35-45 in <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, 29 in <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, 15-20 in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, and a handful each in <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> By the 2000s, with some continued declines and minor local recoveries (as well as more comprehensive surveying) resulted in an estimate of 1500 pairs in Europe, still far less than historic numbers (at least a 30% reduction since the 1950s) and qualifying the species for local <a href="/wiki/Critically_endangered" title="Critically endangered">critically endangered</a> status. Local extinction is probable in many if not all populations yet there is no overarching action plan for conserving the species.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lopez-Lopez_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lopez-Lopez-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> On evidence, populations in core protected areas have increased but fringe areas, important especially to vagrant juveniles, continue to show strong declines and high mortality rates.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> Although listed today by the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">IUCN</a> as breeding species there, the Bonelli's eagle may be extinct as a nesting species in <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">Bosnia and Herzegovina</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> As of 2010, 20 to 22 breeding territories have been found in <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> and it was thought that this population holds about 95% of the remaining Italian population. Sicilian eagles per study were shown to have high adult mortality (10.2%) and at least 17 pairs in 2010 failed to breed altogether.<sup id="cite_ref-Lopez-Lopez_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lopez-Lopez-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vittorio_86-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vittorio-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> In their Spanish stronghold, the species has declined or disappeared in 27 out of 40 provinces since 1980, with over 20% reduction in north and central parts. The coastal sierras of east and south Iberia hold highest European densities at 1 pair per 100–200&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39–77&#160;sq&#160;mi), but once formerly it held a pair per 60&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (23&#160;sq&#160;mi) in the 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sanchez-Alonso_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sanchez-Alonso-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Region_of_Murcia" title="Region of Murcia">Region of Murcia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, the Bonelli's eagle was considered the second most threatened raptor species, behind only the <a href="/wiki/Lesser_kestrel" title="Lesser kestrel">lesser kestrel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Burgos" title="Province of Burgos">Province of Burgos</a> in northern Spain the number of pairs reduced from 25-27 to 10 between 1980 and 1996. Of 100 breeding attempts from 1988 to 1996, only 0.3 were successful and average success rate was only 0.35, despite surplus feeding beginning after 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> From 200 or more pairs in Greece in the early 1980s, the population has fallen to less than 50.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> What was roughly estimated to be about 50 pairs (estimated earlier at up to 100), in Turkey in the late 1980s to the 1990s, has recently been revised based on research to only 20-35 pairs in isolated small pockets.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vaassen_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaassen-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, 28 pairs of Bonelli's eagle were known to be present in 1989 but little information has been obtained from the rest of <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> and from <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>. Israeli populations are estimated to have been halved in size. By 2001, only 15 pairs were known to breed in Israel. Besides the four species that have become <a href="/wiki/Local_extinction" title="Local extinction">locally extinct</a> in Israel, it is likely that the Bonelli's eagle is the most endangered Israeli raptor.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> It was estimated that the maximum number in Asia is likely around 35,000 pairs but it could be well less than half of that. Perhaps the only factor preventing authorities such as the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">IUCN</a> from uplisting Bonelli's eagle to a more severe status is due to lack of extensive research on their population in the Asian range. Strong declines in Asia may be occurring as well.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> A bird survey of a large area of <a href="/wiki/Uttarakhand" title="Uttarakhand">Uttarakhand</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> where the species was historically present failed to find any signs of remaining Bonelli's eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Gujarat" title="Gujarat">Gujarat</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, an analysis from the 1990s determined that the species was increasingly scarce due to human disturbance and logging.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In multiple parts of the range, certainly in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">western Europe</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, Bonelli's eagles face a high degree of persecution by hunters, gamekeepers and pigeon-fanciers. Shooting and poisoning of this species persist extensively into the 21st century.<sup id="cite_ref-Kassinis_58-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kassinis-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> Habitat alteration and destruction (e.g. development of roads, intensified agriculture, irrigation of dry fields) in addition to reduced prey numbers and human disturbance in the nesting area are ongoing and increasing threats everywhere for this eagle.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_5-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> Even human activity such as large quantities of people on holiday has been shown to have a negative effect on this eagle as they may alter their range to avoid such activity.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> From 1990 to 1996, 424 dead Bonelli's eagles in Spain were recorded, 55% died due to electrocution and 26% due to poisoning and shooting. Adults were mainly killed via persecution whereas most juveniles died by electrocution. In <a href="/wiki/Catalonia" title="Catalonia">Catalonia</a> and central Spain, 50% and 86% due to electrocution whereas persecution was more major in <a href="/wiki/Levante,_Spain" title="Levante, Spain">Levante</a> and <a href="/wiki/Green_Spain" title="Green Spain">northern Spain</a> (accounting for 52% and 43% of deaths).<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> Abandonment of territories could not be correlated to interspecific competition but was linked to human influence and persecution.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>, the main threats are thought to be habitat fragmentation and intensifying agriculture. Previously egg-collectors were semi-regularly exacerbating the reduction of the species on Sicily, but this behaviour has seemingly declined mercifully in recent years.<sup id="cite_ref-Lopez-Lopez_8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lopez-Lopez-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vittorio_86-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vittorio-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> Given its relative scarcity in <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>, only a small number of Bonelli's eagles were recovered dead from persecution when compared to other raptors. However, death through shooting and poisoning is surely not sustainable given the low population there.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> Increasing <a href="/wiki/Overhead_power_line" title="Overhead power line">powerline</a> collisions resulting in electrocution from highly dangerous pylons are a major cause of mortality, resulting in unsustainably high population turnover. In one Spanish study area, 56% of juveniles and 13% of adults were killed by electrocution. In France, 44% of radio-tagged post-dispersal juveniles were killed by electrocution.<sup id="cite_ref-Manosa_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manosa-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> Wind farms in Spain are a potential growing source of changed territories and deaths for Bonelli's eagles but they are likely to be less effected locally than golden eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lead_poisoning" title="Lead poisoning">Lead poisoning</a> from bullets in injured small game, which have been associated with high lead levels in eagle feathers in several parts of their range.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> Research from <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">western Europe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Northeastern Africa">northeastern Africa</a> has indicated low genetic diversity in these populations, which cause concerns of a <a href="/wiki/Population_bottleneck" title="Population bottleneck">genetic bottleneck</a> for the species in these former strongholds.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation_efforts">Conservation efforts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bonelli%27s_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Conservation efforts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Research has indicated that the most significant predicted cause to a strong recovery for Bonelli's eagles in Europe would be conservation of appropriate habitats, followed by higher survival rates for territorial and non-territorial eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> It was suggested in 2008 that reducing risk of electric powerline collisions and reducing persecution are the most immediate and significant measures that should be taken to retain Bonelli's eagles in Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-Cheylan_173-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cheylan-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> Research indicated that 99% of avian mortality would be reduced by modifying only 27% of the pylons in areas inhabited by the eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Manosa_207-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manosa-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> As reported by 2015, biologists in coordination with local authorities started to properly insulate dangerous powerlines in green areas in order to help converse this and other threatened birds. It was shown that the local population growth rates increased quickly as a result (from 0.82 to 0.98). However, this study showed an apparent increase of anthropogenic mortality from other causes, such as car collisions, in sync with reduced electrocution.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> It was estimated that for stage of 2008-2014, 0.28 and 0.64 of mortality was still due to electrocution for territorial and non-territorial eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> In further efforts to converse the species locally, Spanish researchers have provided supplemental feedings to these eagles, which may improve their odds of successfully producing young.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
